Naskh — Abrogation
intermediate
15 min
Interpretation of Hadith of Ghaylan: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Analyzes the hadith of Ghaylan and Fayruz regarding keeping limited wives after conversion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that ahad hadith are interpreted in light of the Quran, and evaluate the differing interpretations of the hadith of Ghaylan regarding keeping four wives upon embracing Islam.

Core Idea
Ahad hadith cannot override clear Quranic text; thus, sequential marriages upon conversion remain valid without renewal.

In this lesson, we examine a crucial usul principle: how ahad hadith are treated when they appear to conflict with the Quran. The hadith of Ghaylan, who embraced Islam with ten wives, is interpreted differently by the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools. The Hanafi position, relying on the Quranic limits of four wives, holds that the husband may keep four without renewing contracts, while the Shafi'i requires renewal or adherence to sequence. We will trace the reasoning and the usul principles at play.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two hadith and their apparent commands
Locate the hadith of Ghaylan (keep four) and Fayruz (keep whichever you wish). Note that both are ahad reports.
These are narrated by al-Tirmidhi, Ibn Majah, Ibn Hibban.
2
Understand the Shafi'i interpretation
The Shafi'is require either tajdid (new contract) for simultaneous marriages or keeping the first four for sequential ones, based on the literal command.
They assume the command implies either renewal or sequential priority.
3
Analyze the Hanafi critique of improbability
The Hanafis argue it is improbable the Prophet would address a new Muslim with obscure legal speech, and no report of tajdid exists.
This casts doubt on the Shafi'i literal reading.
4
Apply the Hanafi usul principle: ahad cannot override the Quran
The Quran (4:3, 4:23) explicitly limits valid marriages to four and prohibits combining sisters. These ahad reports must be interpreted in harmony with the Quran.
The Quran is qat'i; ahad are zanni.
5
Derive the Hanafi ruling
Imam Muhammad holds that the husband’s existing contracts remain valid for up to four without renewal; sequential marriages mean only the last is invalid from the start.
In simultaneous marriages, all are invalid and require new contracts.

The hadith is narrated by al-Tirmidhi, Ibn Majah, and Ibn Hibban and authenticated. And the aspect of its improbability is his saying: It is improbable that he (PBUH) would address someone like him (like Ghaylan, newly Muslim, ignorant of rulings) with such precise and obscure speech, especially since no renewal of marriage is reported from him or from others, and if it had occurred it would have been reported. This is another aspect of improbability. (I say)

Even if granted, there is no prevalent motivation to transmit it. If you say: Then you interpret the hadith by the rare? I say: No, rather we say: If what occurred there was rare, then the interpretation is such; otherwise it is such. It only leads to objection if we are certain of the first possibility. (And it may be said: They must interpret simultaneity in Islam as actual simultaneity, as al-Shafi'i (RA) held, because sequentiality in Islam is like apostasy in his view regarding separation, so the marriage of a wife is annulled if she embraced Islam after him even by less than an hour, and similarly the marriage of a wife who embraced Islam after another Muslim woman——(pure) improbability (also). So the criticism of improbability recoils. Then resembling that interpretation is their interpretation of his saying (PBUH) to Fayruz al-Daylami, while he had embraced Islam with two sisters: 'Keep whichever you wish' — narrated by al-Tirmidhi but with another wording (i.e.) keep whichever you wish (by renewal) i.e. by renewing marriage (based on his knowledge) (PBUH) (that he had married them simultaneously). And it was said in al-Mukhtasar: The interpretation here is more improbable because of his saying 'whichever' for it is general, but this has its own issue, because generality only precludes specifying one in the case of sequence, not simultaneity. So the improbability is that same improbability. Perhaps the author of al-Mukhtasar thought they interpret those two interpretations alternately, so there is no doubt it is more improbable. But it is not as he thought; rather they apply it to simultaneity, so the improbability is one. Know that because of this improbability, Imam Muhammad held to choosing in keeping by the previous marriage, and does not make sequentiality in Islam a separator like apostasy such that the improbability that applies to al-Shafi'i would apply. And the excuse for the two shaykhs is that the text of the Book of Allah indicates the obligation of observing the number of four in marriage and the prohibition of exceeding this number. The exceeding is only from the last in the case of sequence and from all in simultaneity. Similarly, combining two sisters is only from the last in the first and from all in the second. So the Quranic text speaks of the invalidity of the marriage of the last in sequence and all in simultaneity at the time of contract. The two hadiths are ahad reports opposing this ruling, so they are not accepted; therefore interpretation is a descent (from the apparent). The conclusion is that they interpret only because of opposition; they are weakened when opposed. If you say: The prohibited is only combining and it only necessitates keeping both, so they must separate one of them not specifically, either separate a specific one or all, so both? I say: The corrupting factor: Firstly, based on this, it would necessitate that the marriage of a Muslim of five or two sisters is contracted and the husband has the choice. Secondly, the discussion is at the time of contract: the marriages of four or one sister are certainly valid because they are free of corruption. If the fifth marriage or the other sister is joined, it becomes corrupt due to the existence of the corrupting factor. If it becomes corrupt from the origin, the last becomes a stranger (ajnabiyyah) in the sight of Allah, so what choice does he have? In the case of simultaneity, the marriage is inseparable from the corrupting factor, so it becomes corrupt at the time of contract, and all become strangers, so he has no choice except in marrying anew. And with what we have established, the narration in al-Mishkat quoted from Sharh al-Sunnah from Nawfal: 'I embraced Islam while I had five women under me, and I had five women under me, so I asked the Prophet (PBUH) and he said: Divorce one and keep four. So I went to the oldest in companionship, barren for sixty years, and divorced her' — an explicit statement that does not accept interpretation by 'the first ones'. Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a detailed Hanafi textual analysis of the hadith of Ghaylan ibn Salamah al-Thaqafi, who embraced Islam with ten wives. The Shafi'is argue that the Prophet's command 'keep four and divorce the rest' must be interpreted either as requiring a new marriage contract (tajdid) if the marriages were simultaneous, or keeping the first four if sequential. The author, following the explanation of Sadr al-Shariah, points out the improbability of the Prophet addressing a newly Muslim with such intricate legal speech, and the lack of reports of tajdid. He refutes the Shafi'i position by arguing that sequentiality in Islam does not automatically dissolve all previous marriages like apostasy. He extends the discussion to the hadith of Fayruz al-Daylami with two sisters, where the Prophet said 'keep whichever you wish'. The author notes that the Hanafi position of Imam Muhammad is that the husband chooses based on the previous marriage without requiring tajdid, because the marriages are only affected at the time of contract; the Quranic prohibition of exceeding four wives or combining sisters invalidates only the excess marriages from the beginning. Ahad reports cannot override the clear Quranic text. The passage concludes by rejecting a report from al-Mishkat that seems to contradict this, arguing that it does not affect the usul principle.

Key Terms
التجديد (al-tajdid): Renewal of the marriage contract; the Shafi'is require this for concurrent marriages to be valid. The Hanafi response is that it is not needed as the original contracts remain valid for up to four.
المعية (al-mu'iyyah): The condition of simultaneity — all marriages occurring at the same time. The Hanafis argue that the hadith does not necessarily assume this, and if it were, all would be invalid and require new contracts.
التعاقب (al-ta'aqub): Sequentiality — marriages occurring one after another. In this case, only the last marriage (exceeding four) is invalid, and the earlier ones remain valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad's position as presented in the text, a man who converts to Islam with five wives (married sequentially) may:
A) Keep any four of the five without renewing the marriage contract
B) Must divorce all five and remarry four anew
C) Keep only the first four in order of marriage
D) Choose one of the five and divorce the rest

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prioritizes the clear Quranic text over ahad hadith; thus, sequential marriages upon conversion remain valid without renewal, while simultaneous marriages require renewal.

Module 14 · Lesson 2

Naskh — Abrogation
intermediate
10 min
Resolving Conflict Between Quran and Ahad Hadith on Marrying Multiple Wives
Dispute on interpreting hadith of Fayruz al-Daylami on multiple marriages

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a decisive Quranic text (qat'i) prevails over ahad reports (zanni) to resolve apparent contradictions in marriage laws.

Core Idea
A decisive Quranic text cannot be overridden by an ahad report.

This passage from 'Fawatih al-Rahamut' presents a classic usul dispute: when a man converts to Islam with more than four wives or two sisters, does the Quranic prohibition (Q 4:3) invalidate the excess marriages immediately, or do the ahad hadiths of Fayruz and Nawfal allow him a choice to keep some? The Hanafi masters argue that the decisive Quranic text (qat'i) must prevail over the probable (zanni) ahad reports. Understanding this hierarchy of evidence is essential for resolving apparent contradictions between primary sources.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conflicting texts
Locate the Quranic verse (an-Nisa:3) and the hadith of Fayruz al-Daylami ('Amsik ayyatahunna shi'ta'). State the apparent contradiction: the Quran limits wives to four; the hadith seems to allow keeping one of two sisters without renewal.
2
Classify each text by thubut and dalala
Determine the epistemological weight: the Quran is qat'i al-thubut (certain transmission) and qat'i al-dalala (clear meaning regarding the number limit). The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) and its dalala is zanni (ambiguous between simultaneity and succession).
Thubut: certainty of transmission. Dalala: clarity of meaning.
3
State the Hanafi rule for resolving qat'i vs zanni conflict
In Hanafi usul, a qat'i text cannot be abrogated or overridden by a zanni text. When they appear to conflict, the zanni text must be interpreted (ta'wil) to align with the qat'i, or if that is impossible, it is set aside.
This rule is derived from the principle that the stronger proof prevails.
4
Apply the rule to the convert's case
Since the Quran decisively prohibits exceeding four wives or combining sisters, any marriage beyond that is invalid at the time of contracting. The hadith of Fayruz is interpreted as referring to renewal of marriage (tajdid), not as giving a choice to keep a void marriage.
This is the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the two shaykhs).
5
Note the intra-Hanafi divergence and tarjih
Imam Muhammad reportedly held a different view, allowing the husband to keep one wife without renewal, based on the hadith. However, the text prefers the majority view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, citing the decisive nature of the Quranic text.
The tarjih (preference) is based on the stronger proof (qat'i) over the weaker (zanni).

الموجب للجمع فسد لوجود المفسد، وإذا فسد من الأصل صارت الأخيرة أجنبية عند الله تعالى، ففي أي شيء يكون الخيار إليه، وفي صورة المعية نكاح ملازم للمفسد ففسد وقت الانعقاد فصارت الكل أجنبيات فلم يبق له الخيار إلا في التزوج تجديداً، وبما قررنا سقط أن الذي في المشكاة» منقولاً عن «شرح السنة عن نوفل قال : أسلمت وتحتي خمس نسوة، وتحتي خمس نسوة، فسألت النبي صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم فقال : فارق واحدة وأمسك أربعاً فعمدت إلى أقدمهن صحبة عاقر منذ ستين سنة ففارقتها مفسر لا يقبل التأويل بالأوائل، وجه السقوط أن التأويل كان تنزلاً، فإن قبل التأويل وإلا يترك العمل لمعارضة الكتاب القاطع، ولقائل أن يقول : الكفار ليسوا مخاطبين بالفروع عند الإمام فخر الإسلام وغيره فغيلان ونوفل لم يكونا قبل مخاطبين بالاقتصار على الأربع، فالأنكحة كلها كانت صحيحة وبعد الإسلام إذ قد توجه خطاب الاقتصار وعدم الجمع لا بد من مفارقة واحدة والتعين تحكم فلزم الخيار فلا معارضة لكتاب الله تعالى، ولك أن تقول : إن هذا غاية الكلام من قبل المشايخ التابعين للإمام محمد رحمه الله، لكن الأمر غير خفي عن الفطن فإنه قد تقدم أن توجه الخطاب بالنهي متفق عليه، ومع ذلك فبعد الإسلام قد توجه النهي عن الاجتماع وهو مسبب من كل من أنكحة كل فيفسد الكل لمقارنتها كل المفسد ، كما إذا نكح المسلم خمساً بعقد، ولا وجه للتخير، ثم إنهم إذا لم يكونوا مخاطبين بالخطابات الشرعية فلا صحة لأنكحتهم من قبل في نظر الشارع، فالزوجات كلهن أجنبيات في نظر الشارع، فيجب تجديد النكاح لا التخيير فالحق أنهم مخاطبون بالعقوبات والمعاملات ونكاح الأوائل منهن صحيح في نصر الشارع لكونها خالية من المفسد، ومأذوناً فيها لقوله: ﴿فَانكِحُوا مَا طَابَ لَكُمْ ﴾ [النساء : ٣] ونكاح الزائدة فاسد لوجود المفسد بالنص القاطع، فلا يقبل المعارضة بخبر الواحد هذا غاية الكلام فتأمل فيه.
The first principle / the book in repelling the last remoteness (renewal of marriage (branch of simultaneity) in marrying the surplus beyond four, and perhaps it only occurred rarely and it is not necessary to transmit the rare, and even if conceded, there is no incentive to transmit it. If you say: So you interpret the hadith as referring to the rare? I say: No, but we say: If what occurred there was rare, then the interpretation is thus, otherwise it is thus, and it would only be problematic if we were certain of the first scenario. (It may be said that they must interpret simultaneity in Islam as instant simultaneity and interpret simultaneity therein as instant simultaneity, as al-Shafi‘i, may Allah have mercy on him, held (because succession in Islam (is like apostasy in his view in causing separation) so the marriage of the wife is annulled if she converted after him even by less than an hour, and likewise the marriage of a wife who converted after another Islam (is far-fetched) purely (also), so the criticism of remoteness retreats backward. Then similar to that interpretation is their interpretation of his statement, peace be upon him and his family and companions, to Fayruz al-Daylami while he had converted while having two sisters: 'Keep whichever of them you wish' — narrated by al-Tirmidhi but with another wording — meaning keep whichever of them you wish with renewal, i.e., with renewal of marriage, based on his knowledge, peace be upon him and his family and companions, that he had married both together. It was said in al-Mukhtasar: The interpretation here is more far-fetched because of his saying 'whichever of them' for it is general, and there is something in that because generality only contradicts the specification of one in the case of succession, not simultaneity, so the remoteness is that remoteness and nothing else. Perhaps the author of al-Mukhtasar claimed that they interpret those two interpretations as alternatives. So there is no doubt that it is more far-fetched, but the matter is not as he thought; rather they interpret it as simultaneity, so the remoteness is one. Know that because of this remoteness, Imam Muhammad held the view of choice in keeping with the previous marriage, and he does not consider succession in Islam as causing separation like apostasy such that the remoteness that applies to al-Shafi‘i would apply. The excuse for the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) is that the text of the Book of Allah indicates the obligation of observing the number of four in marriage and the prohibition of exceeding this number; exceeding is only from the last in the case of succession and from all in simultaneity. Likewise, combining two sisters is only from the last in the first and from all in the second. So the Quranic text explicitly states the invalidity of the marriage of the last in succession and all in simultaneity at the time of contracting, and the two hadiths are ahad reports opposing this ruling, so they are not accepted; thus the interpretation is a concession. The conclusion is that they only interpret due to opposition, and they (the hadiths) collapse when opposed. If you say: What is prohibited is only combination, and it only obligatory by preserving both, so it is necessary to separate one of them not specifically, and either separating a specific one or all, it is not so. I say: The invalidator, first, on this basis, it would follow that the marriage of a Muslim to five or two sisters is contracted and the choice is to the husband. Second, the discussion is at the time of contracting: the marriages of four or to one sister are certainly valid due to absence of invalidator, so when the fifth marriage that violates the required number or the other sister that causes combination occurs, it becomes invalid due to the presence of the invalidator. When it is invalid from the beginning, the last becomes a foreign woman in the sight of Allah, so in what matter does he have a choice? In the case of simultaneity, the marriage is inherently accompanied by the invalidator, so it is invalid at the time of contracting, so all become foreign women, so he has no choice except to marry anew. By what we have established, it is refuted that which is in al-Mishkat, transmitted from Sharh al-Sunnah from Nawfal who said: I converted and had under me five women, so I asked the Prophet, peace be upon him and his family and companions, and he said: 'Separate from one and keep four.' So I went to the oldest in companionship, barren for sixty years, and separated from her. This explicit wording cannot be interpreted by the former. The reason for its refutation is that the interpretation was a concession; if it is interpreted, otherwise the action is abandoned due to opposition to the decisive Book. Someone may say: The disbelievers are not addressed regarding the branches according to Imam Fakhr al-Islam and others, so Ghaylan and Nawfal were not previously addressed to limit to four, so all their marriages were valid, and after Islam, when the command of limiting and prohibition of combination came, a separation of one is necessary, and specification is arbitrary, so choice is necessitated, so there is no opposition to the Book of Allah. You may say: This is the best statement from the later scholars following Imam Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, but the matter is not hidden from the intelligent, for it has already been established that the addressing of the prohibition is agreed upon. Nevertheless, after Islam, the prohibition of combination has come, and it is caused by each of the marriages, so all become invalid due to their accompanying the invalidator, just as if a Muslim marries five with one contract, and there is no possibility of choice. Moreover, if they were not addressed by the legal addresses, then their previous marriages have no validity in the view of the Lawgiver, so all wives are foreign women in the sight of the Lawgiver, so renewal of marriage is required, not choice. The truth is that they are addressed regarding penalties and transactions, and the marriages of the first (women) are valid in the view of the Lawgiver because they are free from the invalidator, and permitted by His saying: 'So marry what is lawful to you' [al-Nisa: 3], and the marriage of the extra is invalid due to the presence of the invalidator by the decisive text, so it cannot be opposed by a single report. This is the best statement, so ponder it.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute about reconciling conflicting evidences: the Quranic verse limiting wives to four (an-Nisa: 3) and ahad hadiths such as that of Fayruz al-Daylami and Nawfal. The author, commenting on Muslim al-Thubut, explains the Hanafi position that ahad reports cannot override a decisive Quranic text. The discussion centers on whether the marriages of a man who converts to Islam while having more than four wives or two sisters are valid and whether he has a choice to keep some. The Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) holds that the Quranic prohibition is decisive, so the excess marriages are invalid from the outset, and the husband must renew the marriage with the permissible number. Imam Muhammad reportedly allowed choice, but the author prefers the view of the two shaykhs, arguing that the ahad hadiths are interpreted as concessions (ta'wil) or set aside. The author refutes objections by stating that disbelievers are addressed regarding some matters and that the Quranic text is conclusive. The passage demonstrates key usul principles: the supremacy of the Quran over ahad hadith, the concept of ijmal and tafsir, and the role of ta'wil in resolving apparent conflicts.

Key Terms
المعية (al-mu‘iyyah): Simultaneity; the state of two marriages being contracted at the same time without succession.
الترتيب (al-tartib): Succession; marriages contracted one after another in order.
خبر الواحد (khabar al-wahid): A report transmitted by one or a few narrators, not reaching the level of tawatur.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, why is the hadith of Fayruz al-Daylami ('Amsik ayyatahunna shi'ta') not accepted as overriding the Quranic limit on wives?
A) Because the hadith is weak (da'if)
B) Because the Quranic text is decisive (qat'i) and ahad reports cannot oppose it
C) Because the hadith is abrogated
D) Because the hadith only applies to the specific companion

Key Takeaway
The decisive Quranic text (qat'i) takes precedence over ahad reports (zanni); apparent conflicts must be resolved by interpreting the weaker evidence to align with the stronger.

Naskh — Abrogation
intermediate
8 min
Bayan Taghyir: Istithna, Shart, and Naskh
Definition and classification of contrary bayan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between bayan taghyir (change of locus), bayan tabdil (change of type), and naskh (abrogation), and explain why naskh is not considered a form of bayan in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
Contrary clarification either changes the locus (istithna), the type (shart), or removes the ruling entirely (naskh) — only removal is not bayan.

In this passage from Sharh Musallam al-Thubut, the commentator clarifies the categories of contrary bayan (bayan mukhalif). Three distinct cases are examined: the conjunct exception (istithna), the conditional clause (shart), and abrogation (naskh). Understanding this classification is crucial for correctly applying the principle that bayan is primarily clarification, not removal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two temporal categories
The passage first divides contrary bayan into conjunct (muqarin) and delayed (mutakhr). Conjunct bayan is immediately attached to the original statement, while delayed bayan may come later. Istithna is conjunct; shart and naskh are delayed.
2
Analyze istithna as taghyir
Istithna is called 'taghyir' because it changes the apparent meaning of the speech by excluding some instances, but the type of ruling remains the same. It only changes the locus (mahall) of the ruling.
The text emphasizes that istithna must be conjunct; delay invalidates it.
3
Analyze shart as tabdil
Qadi Abu Zayd argues that shart is tabdil (substitution) because it creates a conditional relationship that did not exist before. The ruling changes from unconditional to conditional, thus altering its type.
This is a minority Hanafi view; the majority still consider shart as a form of bayan taghyir.
4
Understand naskh outside bayan
Naskh is the removal (raf') of a ruling after it has been established. Since it does not clarify the meaning but removes it, it is not considered bayan.
The text asserts that remaining (baqa') is not part of the implication of speech, so removal does not contradict clarification.
5
Synthesize the distinction
Summarize: istithna changes the scope (taghyir), shart changes the condition (tabdil), naskh changes the existence (removal). Only the first two are bayan.
This classification helps avoid conflating abrogation with specification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والبيان المخالف إما مقارن كالاستثناء وهو بيان تغيير بيان التغيير ما يغير الكلام عن المعنى الحقيقي الظاهر قبل ذكره، لكنه لا يكون إلا مقارناً، ولا يجوز التراخي أصلاً لما مر في عدم جواز تراخي تخصيص العام، فالمقارنة لازمة له ففسر بها وإليه أشار بقوله (ولا يصح موصولاً، وقد مر) في التخصيصات أو متأخر وهو بيان التبديل، وهو النسخ) فإنه تبديل حقيقة، وقيل القائل القاضي الإمام أبو زيد رحمه الله تعالى التبديل هو الشرط) فإنه مبدل لحكم الجزاء، إذ لا حكم فيه أصلاً، بل يحدث حكم تعليقي بين الشرط والجزاء فقد تغير الحكم من نوع إلى نوع أو من وجود إلى عدم من بدء الأمر، وهذا بخلاف الاستثناء، فإنه يبقى الحكم كما كان قبله، لكن يكون على ما بقي بعد الاستثناء، فقد تغير محل الحكم لا نوعه والنسخ خارج عن البيان لأنه رفع بعد تحقق ومفاد الكلام إنما كان التحقق في الجملة ولم يتبدل وإنما لم يبق والبقاء ليس من مدلولات الكلام فتدبر فقد وضح الفرق بين الاستثناء والشرط والنسخ في كون الأول تغييراً والثاني تبديلاً والثالث خارجاً عن البيان
And the contrary bayan is either conjunct (like exception), and it is bayan taghyir – bayan that changes the speech from its apparent real meaning before its mention, but it only occurs conjunct; delay is absolutely not allowed, as previously mentioned regarding disallowing delay of specification of the general. So conjunction is necessary for it, and thus it is interpreted by it. He indicated this by his saying: 'And it is not valid connectedly, and it has passed in the specifications.' Or it is delayed, and it is bayan tabdil, which is naskh – for it is substitution in reality. And it was said – the one who said was Qadi Imam Abu Zayd, may Allah have mercy on him – that substitution is shart (condition), for it substitutes the ruling of the consequence, since there is no ruling originally, but it creates a suspensive ruling between condition and consequence. So the ruling changes from one type to another, or from existence to non-existence from the beginning. This is unlike exception, for the ruling remains as before, but it applies to what remains after the exception; thus the locus of the ruling changes, not its type. And naskh is outside bayan, because it is a removal after realization, and the import of the speech was that realization occurred in sum, and it was not substituted, only it did not remain – and remaining is not among the implications of speech. So reflect. The difference between exception, condition, and naskh is clarified: the first is taghyir, the second is tabdil, and the third is outside bayan.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage explains bayan mukhalif (contrary clarification), which changes the apparent meaning. It has three forms: istithna (exception), shart (condition), and naskh (abrogation). Istithna is a change (taghyir) that is conjunct with the original statement; it cannot be delayed. The example is 'except' clauses. The ruling remains the same in type but applies to a reduced scope. Shart is considered by Qadi Abu Zayd as tabdil (substitution) because it creates a new conditional relationship, changing the ruling from unconditional to conditional. Naskh is outside the definition of bayan because it removes a ruling after it has been established; it is not merely a clarification but an abrogation. The key distinction: exception changes the locus of the ruling; condition changes the type of ruling; abrogation removes the ruling entirely.

Key Terms
الاستثناء (al-istithna): Exception; a conjunct particle that changes the locus of the ruling by excluding some cases.
الشرط (al-shart): Condition; a clause that changes the ruling from unconditional to conditional, considered tabdil.
النسخ (al-naskh): Abrogation; removal of a ruling after it has been established; outside the definition of bayan.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi classification in this passage, which of the following is NOT considered a form of bayan?
A) Istithna (exception)
B) Shart (condition)
C) Naskh (abrogation)
D) All are forms of bayan

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, contrary bayan includes istithna (taghyir) and shart (tabdil), but excludes naskh which is abrogation, not clarification.

Distinguishing Istithna, Sharat, and Naskh in Hanafi Usul
Definition and comparison of istithna, sharat, and naskh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between istithna, sharat, and naskh by identifying how each affects the ruling.

Core Idea
Istithna changes the subject, sharat changes the ruling type, naskh removes the ruling — each is a distinct legal operation.

In Hanafi usul, not every change to a ruling is the same. Exception (istithna), condition (sharat), and abrogation (naskh) each alter the legal effect but in fundamentally different ways. This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut sharpens your ability to classify these operations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
Recognize istithna (exception), sharat (condition), and naskh (abrogation) as distinct tools that modify a ruling.
They are often confused; knowing the difference is critical for correct istinbat.
2
Understand the nature of change for each
Istithna changes the locus (mahall) of the ruling but not its type. Sharat changes the ruling type by making it contingent. Naskh removes the ruling entirely after it was established.
The passage calls sharat 'tabdil' (substitution) and naskh 'raf' (removal).
3
Relate each to bayan
Istithna is a form of bayan (clarification). Sharat is considered substitution. Naskh is outside bayan because it cancels rather than explains.
This classification affects how you interpret texts.
4
Apply the distinction to scenarios
Given a textual operation, determine whether it is istithna, sharat, or naskh by checking if the subject changes, the ruling type changes, or the ruling is removed.
Practice with examples from Qur'an and Sunnah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل الأول/ الكتاب (، ، إلا الحقيقي الظاهر قبل ذكره، لكنه لا يكون إلا مقارناً، ولا يجوز التراخي أصلاً لما مر في عدم جواز تراخي تخصيص العام، فالمقارنة لازمة له ففسر بها وإليه أشار بقوله (ولا يصح موصولاً، وقد مر) في التخصيصات أو متأخر وهو بيان التبديل، وهو النسخ) فإنه تبديل حقيقة، وقيل القائل القاضي الإمام أبو زيد رحمه الله تعالى التبديل هو الشرط) فإنه مبدل لحكم الجزاء، إذ لا حكم فيه أصلاً، بل يحدث حكم تعليقي بين الشرط والجزاء فقد تغير الحكم من نوع إلى نوع أو من وجود إلى عدم من بدء الأمر، وهذا بخلاف الاستثناء، فإنه يبقى الحكم كما كان قبله، لكن يكون على ما بقي بعد الاستثناء، فقد تغير محل الحكم لا نوعه والنسخ خارج عن البيان لأنه رفع بعد تحقق ومفاد الكلام إنما كان التحقق في الجملة ولم يتبدل وإنما لم يبق والبقاء ليس من مدلولات الكلام فتدبر فقد وضح الفرق بين الاستثناء والشرط والنسخ في كون الأول تغييراً والثاني تبديلاً والثالث خارجاً عن البيان
The first principle / The Book. Except the real apparent before its mention, but it is only concurrent, and delay is not permissible at all because of what passed on the impermissibility of delaying the specification of the general. So concurrence is necessary for it, thus it was explained by it, and he pointed to it by his saying (and it is not valid as connected, and it has passed) in the specifications, or delayed and it is the clarification of substitution, which is abrogation, for it is substitution in reality. And it was said, the speaker al-Qadi Imam Abu Zaid, may Allah have mercy on him, substitution is the condition, for it changes the ruling of the consequence, since there is no ruling in it originally, but rather it brings about a suspensive ruling between the condition and the consequence, so the ruling has changed from one type to another or from existence to non-existence from the beginning. And this is unlike exception, for the ruling remains as it was before, but it is upon what remains after the exception, so the locus of the ruling has changed, not its type. And abrogation is outside clarification because it is a removal after realization, and the import of the speech was only realization in a general way and it did not change, but it did not remain, and remaining is not part of the implications of the speech. So reflect. So the difference between exception, condition, and abrogation has become clear: the first is change, the second is substitution, and the third is outside clarification.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a definition and comparison of three legal tools: exception (istithna), condition (sharat), and abrogation (naskh). The author clarifies that exception only changes the subject of the ruling without changing the type of ruling, condition changes the ruling by making it contingent, and abrogation nullifies the ruling entirely and is not a form of clarification. The commentary follows the order of the source text, first discussing exception and its requirement of concurrence, then condition as a 'substitution' for the ruling of the consequence, and finally abrogation as external to the original speech. The text emphasizes that abrogation does not change the original meaning but removes the ruling after it was established.

Key Terms
استثناء (istithna'): A legal tool that restricts the subject of a ruling without changing the type of ruling; the ruling applies to what remains after the exception.
شرط (sharat): A condition that brings about a suspensive ruling, changing the ruling from one type to another or from existence to non-existence from the beginning.
نسخ (naskh): Abrogation: removal of a ruling after it has been realized, not considered a form of clarification because it cancels rather than explains.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Exception changes the ruling type, while abrogation changes the subject.

C) Both are identical in function.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, exception modifies the subject, condition changes the ruling type, and abrogation removes the ruling — each is a distinct operation with different effects on legal derivation.

Naskh — Abrogation

The Definition of Naskh (Abrogation) in Hanafi Usul
Introducing the section on naskh with linguistic and technical definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the linguistic and technical definitions of naskh and distinguish them from related concepts like munāsakha and tanāsukh.

Core Idea
Naskh is the raising of a legal ruling by a later evidence—not a change of text but a change of ruling.

This passage opens the section on naskh in the study of usul al-fiqh. The author begins with the linguistic ambiguity of the term and its technical meaning in Islamic jurisprudence. Understanding this definition is crucial because naskh is a key mechanism by which the Lawgiver adjusts rulings over time.

Guided Path
1
Learn the two linguistic meanings
Identify that 'naskh' linguistically means 'removal' (izāla) and 'transfer' (naql). Memorize both meanings.
2
Note the schools of thought on linguistic usage
Recognize the four views: homonym (ishtirāk), literal in one and metaphorical in the other, literal in the other and metaphorical in the first, or a common term (tawātu').
This difference has practical consequence only when interpreting the Lawgiver's speech.
3
Distinguish related terms
Distinguish naskh from munāsakha (transfer of inheritance shares) and tanāsukh (transmigration of souls). These share the root but have different applications.
Derived forms: munāsakha and tanāsukh.
4
Memorize the technical definition
The technical definition: 'raising of a legal ruling by a later evidence.' Note the key components: raising (raf'), legal ruling (hukm sharʿī), and later evidence (dalīl sharʿī muta'akhkhir).
This definition excludes natural termination and postponement.
5
Understand the exclusions
The definition implicitly excludes: raising by death (the ruling person dies), sleep, forgetfulness, and postponement (like 'pray' without immediate obligation).
These are not naskh because the ruling is not lifted by a later evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب في النسخ وإنكاره معنيي الذي هو بيان التبديل، وإنما أفرده لكثرة مسائله ومباحثه (وهو لغة الإزالة والنقل الظاهر منه الاشتراك، وقيل : حقيقة في الأول مجاز في الثاني، وقيل: بالعكس، وقيل: بالتواطىء (ومنه المناسخة) لنقل السهام الموروثة من وارث الميت إلى وارثه (والتناسخ) انتقال الروح من بدن إلى آخر ، وقيل : لا يتعلق بهذا الخلاف عرض وقيل فائدته إذا وقع في كلام الشارع على أيهما يحمل (و) هو (اصطلاحاً فقيل: رفع الشارع الحكم الشرعي) زاد ابن الحاجب بدليل شرعي متأخر أخرج بالأول رفعه بالموت والنوم والغفلة، وبالثاني نحو : صل
Chapter on abrogation (naskh) and its denial? The meaning of which is the explanation of substitution. He devoted it separately due to the abundance of its issues and discussions. (It) linguistically is removal and transfer; the apparent [meaning] from it is that it is a homonym (ishtirāk). And it is said: it is literal in the first and metaphorical in the second. And it is said: the opposite. And it is said: it is a common term (tawātu'). From it is 'al-munāsakha' for the transfer of inherited shares from the heir of the deceased to his heir, and 'al-tanāsukh' the transmigration of the soul from one body to another. And it is said: There is no practical consequence attached to this difference; and it is said: its benefit is when it occurs in the speech of the Lawgiver as to which meaning it is interpreted. And (it) technically it is said: The Lawgiver's raising of a legal ruling. Ibn al-Hājib added: by a later legal evidence. The first excludes raising by death, sleep, and forgetfulness, and the second excludes like 'pray'.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the section on naskh (abrogation) in usul al-fiqh. The author begins by noting the linguistic ambiguity of 'naskh': it can mean 'removal' (izāla) or 'transfer' (naql). Some say it is homonymous between these two, others that one is literal and the other metaphorical, and yet others that it is a common term. Two related terms are derived: 'munāsakha' (transfer of inheritance shares) and 'tanāsukh' (transmigration of souls). The author remarks that this linguistic dispute has no practical benefit unless the term occurs in the Lawgiver's speech. Then he gives the technical definition of naskh in fiqh: the raising (raf') of a legal ruling (hukm sharʿī) by a later evidence (dalīl sharʿī muta'akhkhir), as added by Ibn al-Hājib. This definition excludes natural termination of a ruling (e.g., by death) and postponement (like 'pray' without immediate obligation). The definition is incomplete in the text, but the standard Hanafi understanding is that naskh abrogates a ruling while leaving the text intact.

Key Terms
النسخ (al-naskh): Linguistically: removal or transfer. Technically: the Lawgiver's raising of a legal ruling by a later evidence.
الإزالة (al-izāla): Removal; one of the linguistic meanings of naskh.
النقل (al-naql): Transfer; another linguistic meaning of naskh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the technical definition given in the passage, which of the following is an example of naskh?
A) A ruling ceasing due to the death of the Prophet.
B) The abrogation of a verse by a later verse.
C) A person forgetting a ruling.
D) The postponement of an obligation until later.

Key Takeaway
Naskh is a legislative process by which a later revealed evidence lifts an earlier ruling, leaving the text intact.

Naskh — Abrogation
intermediate
6 min
Defining Naskh: Removal or Expiry?
Linguistic and terminological definitions of naskh with objections and replies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi definition of naskh and distinguish it from other definitions in terms of removal (raf') versus expiry (intiha' al-amad).

Core Idea
Naskh in Hanafi usul is not removal of an eternal command but revelation of its time-bound term.

Abrogation (naskh) is a fundamental concept in usul al-fiqh, often misunderstood as a change in divine will. This lesson parses the linguistic and terminological definitions given by Hanafi and other scholars, addresses key objections, and clarifies the Hanafi position that naskh is the expiry of a ruling's term, not the removal of an eternal command.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic definitions
Examine the linguistic meanings of naskh (removal and transfer) and the debate over whether it is homonymous, literal, or metaphorical. Note the derived terms munakhasa and tanasukh.
The linguistic discussion sets the stage for the terminological definitions.
2
Parse the terminological definitions
List the four main terminological definitions: (1) removal of a legal ruling (with Ibn al-Hajib's addition), (2) text indicating expiry of term (attributed to jurists), (3) expression indicating negation of the condition of continuation (Imam al-Haramayn), (4) discourse indicating removal with delay (al-Ghazali).
Pay attention to how each definition differs in scope and conditions.
3
Analyze the objections and responses
Understand the objection that the definition via removal cannot apply to abrogation of recitation or to an eternal ruling. Study the Hanafi response: removal is of the connection (ta'alluq) to the mukallaf, not of the ruling itself.
This step is crucial to grasping the Hanafi metaphysical basis.
4
Resolve the dispute between definitions
Note the distinction between definitions based on removal vs. expiry. Ibn al-Hajib considers the dispute verbal: 'removal' means cessation of connection, which is equivalent to expiry of the term.
The key is that naskh presupposes the first ruling was time-bound from eternity.
5
Apply to a concrete example
Consider the abrogation of the qibla direction (from Jerusalem to Kaaba). Explain how the Hanafi view explains this without implying a change in God's knowledge.
This application solidifies the theoretical definition.

Chapter on Abrogation and its denial. Its linguistic meaning: removal and transfer. The apparent meaning is homonymy. Some say it is literal in the first and metaphorical in the second; others say the opposite; others say it is a univocal term. From it comes 'al-munakhasa' for the transfer of inherited shares from the heir of the deceased to his heir, and 'al-tanasukh' for the transfer of the soul from one body to another. Some say no practical difference attaches to this dispute; others say its benefit is when it occurs in the speech of the Lawgiver, which meaning is intended. Terminologically: some said: the Lawgiver's removal of a legal ruling. Ibn al-Hajib added: by a later legal proof. By the first part, removal by death, sleep, and forgetfulness is excluded. By the second, like 'pray until the end of the month'; but there is no need for this because the first is non-existence due to lack of capacity, and the second is non-existence due to termination. This is in the Hashiya. It may be said: obligation on the mukallaf is absolutely established, and it is certainly removed by death, and it is not removed except by the Lawgiver's removal necessarily, so a condition to exclude it is needed. Also, conditions are to show what is excluded. Thus, the removal of original permissibility, if realized, is excluded because it is not addressed (i.e., because of a discourse related to it such that a legal ruling is realized). Also, every specification is excluded, because it is repelling the ruling from the beginning, not removal after realization. If you say: the definition does not apply to abrogation of recitation, for the ruling is not removed by it, he said: abrogation of recitation reverts to abrogation of its rulings: permissibility of praying with it, prohibition of touching by one in minor impurity, menstruating, or junub, and reciting them, and that recitation is a cause for great reward, and memorizing it merits immense virtue, etc. It is objected: the ruling is eternal in your view, so its removal is inconceivable, because what is established as eternal is impossible to cease, as explained elsewhere. Since its removal is not valid, then the term 'abrogation' does not apply that it is removal; thus the definition is a definition by what is incompatible. The answer: by removal is not meant the removal of the eternal discourse from reality, but rather the removal of the connection, i.e., the connection of the ruling and discourse to the mukallaf as an actual obligation, such that he becomes obligated for the act that without (i.e., without the removal) would have continued. Understand. Some said (attributed to the jurists): it is the text indicating the expiry of the ruling's term; there is no need to add the condition of delay because expiry implies it. Imam al-Haramayn said: the expression indicating the appearance of the negation of the condition for the continuation of the first ruling. Imam Hujjat al-Islam (may his secret be sanctified) said: the discourse indicating the removal of the ruling established by the first discourse in a way that without it it would be established, with delay; the last condition is only for clarification and explanation, not for exclusion. If you say: the statement of a narrator 'the ruling of such-and-such was abrogated' is excluded, for it is not a text; and also an action is excluded. He said: the statement of a narrator 'the ruling of such-and-such was abrogated' is not a text and does not indicate by itself; rather, it is evidence for it, so its exclusion is fine, like the action of the Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him and his Companions and grant them peace), for it is not a text indicating expiry by itself; rather, it discloses it by the indication that the action of the Messenger, with God's approval, cannot be without. It is objected: the text is a proof of abrogation, not abrogation itself, so the definition does not apply; it is a definition by what is incompatible. The answer: we do not concede that it is a proof of abrogation; rather, it is abrogation itself. Just as a ruling is nothing but 'do', so abrogation is nothing but 'do not' customarily. Reflect, indicating that derivation would not be valid unless one concedes that there are two terminologies in the Hashiya. Then this answer is valid only for internal speech, and applying 'text' to it is far-fetched; even more far-fetched is applying 'word'; and describing it as 'indicator' is inconsistent, for it is the thing indicated, as used by later scholars, unless one concedes that 'abrogation' is also applied to the word, as indicated by his saying 'customarily'. So reflect. Then this definition is based on the first ruling being temporally limited to a time in which the second ruling appears in His knowledge; thus there is no removal, but rather it is a clarification of the term that was set for it. This is unlike the first definition, which is based on its being temporally unlimited, but rather absolute, removed by abrogation. So there is a dispute between the two definers. Ibn al-Hajib said: the dispute is verbal, because by 'removal' we mean the cessation of the connection that was thought to continue before the arrival of the abrogator; and that is what is meant by the expiry of the ruling's term. The matter does not come down to that, because the eternity of the ruling precludes removal but not expiry, because expiry is nothing but the non-existence of something after its term; that is removal, which is precluded by eternity. So abrogation is nothing but the expiry of the ruling at a term.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on naskh (abrogation) in usul al-fiqh. It begins by defining naskh linguistically: it means removal (izala) and transfer (naql), and discusses whether it is homonymous, literal, or metaphorical. The terminological definitions are then given from various scholars: (1) removal of a legal ruling by the Lawgiver (with Ibn al-Hajib's addition of 'by a later legal proof'); (2) the text indicating the expiry of the ruling's term (attributed to the jurists); (3) the expression indicating the negation of the condition for the continuation of the first ruling (Imam al-Haramayn); (4) the discourse indicating the removal of the ruling established by the first discourse, with delay (Imam al-Ghazali). Objections are raised: the definition via removal does not apply to abrogation of recitation, and it implies removal of an eternal ruling, which is impossible. The Hanafi response distinguishes between removal of the ruling itself and removal of its connection (ta'alluq) to the mukallaf. Another objection: if naskh is defined as a text, then the statement of a narrator or an action is excluded because they are not texts. The author replies that customarily, the abrogating command ('do not') is itself the naskh, not just its proof. The passage concludes by noting that the definition based on expiry presupposes the ruling was time-bound from the start, so naskh is merely revealing that time limit, not removing an eternal ruling. Ibn al-Hajib considers the dispute verbal.

Key Terms
النسخ (al-naskh): Terminologically: the Lawgiver's removal of a legal ruling by a later legal proof, or the expiry of the ruling's term.
الرفع (al-raf'): Removal; in the context of naskh, it means the removal of the connection (ta'alluq) of the ruling to the mukallaf, not the removal of the ruling itself from divine knowledge.
انتهاء الأمد (intiha' al-amad): The expiry of the term; the ruling was intended to last only until a specified time, which is revealed by the abrogator.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi definition presented in the text, what does naskh actually remove?
A) The ruling itself from divine knowledge
B) The connection (ta'alluq) of the ruling to the mukallaf
C) The possibility of the ruling being eternal
D) The text of the abrogated ruling

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, naskh is the revelation of the expiry of a ruling's term, not the removal of an eternal command.

Naskh — Abrogation
advanced
12 min
Definition of Naskh: Removal or Clarification?
Exposition of multiple definitions and the raf'/bayan debate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two main definitions of naskh (abrogation) in Hanafi usul—removal (raf') and clarification (bayan)—and explain the Hanafi response to the objection that naskh implies a change in God's eternal knowledge.

Core Idea
Naskh is clarification of a preordained term, not removal of an eternal ruling.

This passage explores the foundational definition of naskh (abrogation) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The central question is whether abrogation involves the removal (raf') of a previously established ruling or merely the clarification (bayan) of its preordained duration. The Hanafi school, emphasizing God's eternal knowledge, prefers the latter. We will navigate the arguments, objections, and responses presented in this commentary on Muslim al-Thubut.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its definitions
Read the passage to list the various definitions of naskh: as a text indicating termination of a ruling's term; as an utterance showing absence of continuation condition; as a command indicating removal with deferment. Note that the core debate is between 'raf'' (removal) and 'bayan' (clarification).
2
Contrast the two main definitions
First definition: naskh is removal (raf') of a ruling after its establishment. Second definition: naskh is clarification (bayan) of the preordained time limit. Hanafis lean toward bayan because the ruling in God's knowledge is eternal and cannot be removed.
3
Analyze the Hanafi objection to raf'
Objection: if the ruling is eternal (qadim), it cannot be removed (ma'dum), so defining naskh as removal contradicts the attribute of qidam. The response is that 'removal' refers to removal of the relation (talluq) between the ruling and the mukallaf, not the ruling itself.
4
Present the Hanafi response and analogy
The Hanafi response uses the analogy of death: a person dies by his appointed term, not by the killing. Killing is merely a sign. Similarly, naskh reveals the term of the ruling. This supports the bayan definition.
5
Synthesize the verbal vs substantive dispute
Ibn al-Hajib calls it a verbal dispute; the author's father (Bahr al-Ulum) argues it is not even a real dispute. The preferred position: naskh is bayan from God's perspective and appears as raf' to us. Both definitions are correct from their respective angles.

قال ابن الحاجب: الخلاف لفظي، لأن مرادنا بالرفع زوال التعلق المظنون استمراره (قبل ورود (الناسخ وهو المراد بانتهاء، أمد الحكم، وليس القرار إليه، لأن قدم الحكم يأبى الرفع دون الانتهاء، لأن الانتهاء ليس إلا عدم وجود شيء بعد الأمد وهو الرفع، ويأبى عنه القدم فإذن ليس النسخ إلا انتهاء الحكم إلى أمد معين، وهو ارتفاع التعلق المظنون بقاؤه (فيؤول النسخ إلى التخصيص) أي يكون في الأزمان مثله في الأفراد والحق أنه خلاف معنوي، وتحقيقه أن الخطاب المطلق النازل (في علمه تعالى : هل كان متناولاً للكل أي كان مقيد بالدوام فكان) النسخ رفعاً) لهذا الحكم المقيد بالدوام. ولا يلزم التكاذب، لأن الإنشاء لا يحتمل الكذب وإنما يرفع الثاني الأول (أو) كان الخطاب في علمه تعالى (مخصصاً بالبعض من الأزمنة وهو الزمان الذي ورد فيه النسخ، لكن لم ينزل التقييد عند نزول المنسوخ (فكان) النسخ (بياناً ) لهذا الأمد المقيد به الحكم عند الله تعالى، فالمعرف بالرفع ذهب إلى الأول وبيان الأمد إلى الثاني والأول) أي كون المنسوخ مرتفعاً بالناسخ لولاه لبقي كالقتل عند المعتزلة فإنهم يقولون : المقتول كأن حياته قد ارتفع بالقتل لولا القتل لبقي حياً (والثاني) أي كون حكم المنسوخ مقيداً والناسخ بيان القيد (كالقتل عندنا معشر أهل السنة والجماعة، فإن المقتول ميت بأجله عندنا، وإذا جاء الأجل لا يستأخر ولا يستقدم، والقتل إنما هو علامة الأجل، ولولا القتل لمات لمجيء أجله أقول : يؤيد مجيء الثاني أن التشريع للضرورة كتزويج (الأخت في شريعة آدم عليه السلام إلى نوح عليه السلام، فإنه لم يكن إذ ذاك نساء غير الأخوات إنما يصح بقدرها ، فلا يتعلق بالكل) وفيه أنه لا تأييد إذ من الجائز أن يكون ابتداء شرعا الحكم لهذه الضرورات، لكن يكون تشريعاً دائمياً، كيف وأنه قد بقي إلى زمان نوح عليه السلام مع أنه قد تكثر النساء في البين غير الأخوات، وأيضاً: أن يكون شرع ما شرع للضرورة مؤبداً إرادة أن ينسخه عند انتفاء الضرورة، فتدبر (ويؤيد الأول أن النهي للدوام فيوجب التعلق مستمراً) فليس نسخه إلا رفعه فتدبر) قال مطلع الأسرار الإلهية والدي قدس سره : الأصفى ليس جعل النزاع معنوياً على هذا الوجه صحيحاً ولا ضرورة ملجئة إليه أيضاً، فإنه ليس بين الفريقين نزاع أصلاً، وكيف يصح هذا، فإنه يلزم على كل أن يحكموا على الله تعالى بأمر لم يهد إليه الدليل ولا حكم به البديهة أيضاً، وليس كل الأحكام مؤقتاً في علم الله تعالى عند أحد ولا الكل مؤبداً عند أحد، فلا فلا يتمكن أحد يصح من إحدى الدعويين مطلقاً، وأيضاً قائلو بيان الأمد جوزوا نسخ المؤقت قبل مجيء وقته، ولا يمكن هذا إلا إذا كان ،رفعاً، بل الحق أن الحكم سواء كان مقيداً بقيد التأبيد أم مطلقاً عنه أم بوقت لم ينزل التقييد به أو نزل له عمر عند الله تعالى إلى أجل معين مقدر البتة، والله سبحانه يعلم هذا الأجل، فإذا جاء ذلك الأجل أنزل حكماً آخر وارتفع الحكم الأول من البين، فالحكم ميت بأجله بإماتة الله سبحانه، وظهور الإماتة ليس إلا بهذا الرفع، فمن نظر إلى الأول عرف بانتهاء أمد الحكم المقدر عند الله تعالى ومن نظر إلى الثاني عرفه برفعه وقول الإمام فخر الإسلام رضي الله تعالى عنه : وهو في حق صاحب الشرع بيان محض لمدة الحكم المطلق الذي كان معلوماً عند الله تعالى إلا أنه أطلقه فصار ظاهره البقاء في حق البشر، فكان تبديلاً في حقنا بياناً محضاً في حق صاحب الشرع ينادي على ما ذكرنا. وقال في «البديع»: وإذا كان في النسخ جهتان صح التعريف بكل واحد منهما، وهذا أيضاً يرشدك إلى ما قلنا، ولا تظنن أنه يلزم من كلام هذا الحبر الإمام تعدد الحق فإنه بالنظر إلى صاحب الشرع شيء مقيداً
To the end of the month, and there is no need for it because the first is negation due to lack of capacity, and the second is negation by a limit, as in 'al-Hashiyah'. And it may be said: the obligation upon the mukallaf is established absolutely, and it is removed by death definitely, and it is not removed except by the Legislator's removal necessarily, so a qualifier is needed to exclude it. Also, qualifiers are to show what is excluded. (Thus the removal of the permissible original, if it occurs, is excluded because it is not a command) i.e., due to a command related to it such that a legal ruling is realized. (And) is excluded (every) specification, because it is a repulsion of the ruling from the beginning, not a removal after realization. If you say: the definition does not apply to abrogation of recitation, because the ruling is not removed by it. He said: abrogation of recitation refers to abrogation of (its rulings) regarding permissibility of praying with it, prohibition of touching by one in ritual impurity, menstruating woman, and junub, and their recitation, and that recitation is a cause for great reward, and memorizing it necessitates immense virtue, etc. (And it was objected: the ruling is eternal according to you, so its removal is inconceivable, because what is established as eternal cannot cease to exist, as clarified in another discipline. Since its removal is not valid, it cannot be said about its abrogation that it is removed, so the definition defines by what is opposite. (The answer): by 'removal' is not meant removal of the eternal command from reality, but rather removal of relation, i.e., the relation of the ruling and command to the mukallaf in a definitive way, so that he becomes obligated for the act which without it (i.e., without the removal) would have remained and continued, so understand. (And it was said) and attributed to the jurists: it is the text indicating the termination of the term of the ruling, and there is no need to add the condition of deferment because termination implies it. And Imam al-Haramayn said: the utterance indicating the manifestation of the absence of the condition for the continuation of the first ruling. And Imam Hujjat al-Islam, may his secret be sanctified, said: the command indicating the removal of the ruling established by the first command, in a manner that without it it would remain, with its deferment, and the last condition is only for clarification and explanation, not for exclusion. If you say: it excludes the narrator's statement 'the ruling of such-and-such is abrogated', because it is not a text, and similarly the action is excluded. He said: (The narrator's statement 'the ruling of such-and-such is abrogated' is not a text nor indicative by itself, but rather is its evidence, so its exclusion is fine, like the action of the Prophet, his family, and his companions, peace be upon them, because it is not a text indicating termination by itself, but rather reveals it by the context that the Prophet's action with God's approval cannot be otherwise. (And it was objected: the text is evidence of abrogation, not its essence, so it does not apply to it, so it is a definition by what is opposite. (The answer): we do not accept that it is evidence of abrogation; rather it is its very essence. For just as the ruling is only 'do', so abrogation is only 'do not' customarily. So reflect, indicating that derivation would be invalid then unless it is conceded that there are two terminologies in the Hashiyah. Then this answer is valid only regarding internal speech, and applying 'text' to it is far-fetched, and even more far-fetched is applying 'utterance', and it rejects describing it as indicative, since it is the indicated meaning according to later scholars, unless one concedes applying abrogation to the utterance also, as his word 'customarily' suggests, so ponder. (Then this definition is based on the first ruling being (temporal) with a time in which the second ruling appears in His knowledge, so there is no removal; rather it is clarification of the term with which it was timed. This is contrary to the first definition, which is based on it being non-temporal, but absolute, removed by abrogation. Thus between the two definitions there is a disagreement. Ibn al-Hajib said: the disagreement is verbal, because by 'removal' we mean the removal of the relation whose continuation was presumed, before the appearance of the abrogator, and that is what is meant by the termination of the term of the ruling. And the decision is not up to him, because the eternity of the ruling precludes removal without termination, because termination is only non-existence of something after the term, and that is removal, and eternity precludes it. So abrogation is only the termination of the ruling at a specific term, which is the removal of the relation whose continuation was presumed. (Thus abrogation reduces to specification) i.e., it is similar in times to specification in individuals. The truth is that it is a meaningful disagreement. Its verification: the absolute command revealed (in His knowledge: was it encompassing everything, i.e., was it restricted by perpetuity? Then abrogation is removal of this perpetuity-restricted ruling. And mutual contradiction does not follow, because creation (insha') does not admit falsehood; rather the second removes the first. (Or) the command in His knowledge was (specific to some times, namely the time when abrogation occurred, but the restriction was not revealed when the abrogated ruling was revealed. (Then) abrogation is (clarification) of this term with which the ruling is restricted according to God. Thus the definer by removal went with the first, and the definer by term clarification went with the second. The first, i.e., that the abrogated ruling is removed by the abrogator; without it, it would remain, like killing according to the Mu'tazila: they say the killed person's life is removed by killing; without killing he would remain alive. And the second, i.e., that the abrogated ruling is restricted and the abrogator clarifies the restriction, like killing according to us, the community of Ahl al-Sunnah: the killed person dies by his term according to us. When the term comes, it is not delayed nor advanced. Killing is only a sign of the term; without killing he would die at his term. I say: the second is supported by the fact that legislation for necessity, like marriage with (one's sister) in the law of Adam (peace be upon him) until Noah (peace be upon him), when there were no women besides sisters, is only valid to the extent of necessity, so it does not pertain to all. But there is no support, because it is possible that the ruling was initially legislated for these necessities but as permanent legislation. How so? For it remained until the time of Noah even though there became many women besides sisters. Also, it is possible to legislate what is legislated for necessity as permanent with the intention to abrogate it when necessity ceases, so ponder. (And the first is supported by the fact that prohibition is for perpetuity, so it necessitates continuous relation, so its abrogation is only its removal, so ponder.) Said the author of Miftah al-Asrar al-Ilahiyyah, my father, may his secret be sanctified: the purest is that making the dispute substantive in this way is not correct, nor is there necessity forcing it. For there is no dispute between the two groups at all. How can this be correct? It would require each to judge concerning God something not guided by evidence nor ruled by intuition. Not every ruling is temporal in God's knowledge according to anyone, nor is all eternal according to anyone. So no one can claim either position absolutely. Also, those who say clarification of term allowed abrogation of a temporal ruling before its time, but this is only possible if it is removal. Rather the truth is that the ruling, whether restricted by perpetuity or absolute or with a time not revealed or revealed, has a life with God to a specific determined term. God, may He be exalted, knows this term. When that term comes, He reveals another ruling and the first ruling is removed from between. So the ruling dies by its term by God's causing death, and the manifestation of causing death is only through this removal. So whoever looks at the first recognizes by the termination of the ruling's term as determined by God, and whoever looks at the second recognizes by its removal. And the saying of Imam Fakhr al-Islam, may God be pleased with him: it is, with respect to the Lawgiver, pure clarification of the duration of the absolute ruling that was known to God, except that He revealed it absolutely, so its apparent meaning is continuation with respect to humans. So it is a change with respect to us, pure clarification with respect to the Lawgiver, calling out to what we mentioned. And it is said in 'al-Badi'': when abrogation has two aspects, definition by each of them is correct. This also guides you to what we said. And do not think that from the speech of this great scholar multiple truths follow, for with respect to the Lawgiver it is one thing, restricted.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion on the definition of naskh (abrogation) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author, through his commentary on the matn (Muslim al-Thubut), presents multiple definitions and addresses objections. The central debate is whether naskh is 'رفع' (removal of a ruling) or 'بيان' (clarification of a preordained term). The first definition, typical of the earlier scholars, defines naskh as a text indicating the termination of a ruling's duration. The second, from Imam al-Haramayn, defines it as an utterance showing the absence of the condition for the ruling's continuation. Imam al-Ghazali defines it as a command indicating removal of a previously established ruling with deferment. The author then responds to objections: e.g., that the ruling is eternal and cannot be removed; the answer is that removal means removal of the relation between the ruling and the mukallaf, not the ruling's essence. Another objection is that a narrator's statement 'this ruling is abrogated' is not a text; the response is that customarily such a statement is itself the abrogation, like the action of the Prophet. The passage then contrasts two underlying positions: one holds that the abrogated ruling was originally unqualified and perpetual, so abrogation is removal; the other holds that it was always time-bound in God's knowledge, so abrogation is clarification. The analogy of killing is used: Mu'tazila view killing as removing life, while Ahl al-Sunnah see it as a sign of the preordained term. The author's father (Bahr al-Ulum) argues that the dispute is merely verbal—both sides are correct from different perspectives. Imam Fakhr al-Islam states that for the Lawgiver it is clarification, while for us it appears as replacement. The ultimate conclusion is that naskh has two aspects: removal from our perspective and clarification from God's perspective. The author urges reflection.

Key Terms
نسخ (naskh): The text or command indicating the termination of a ruling's duration or its removal, depending on perspective. Two main definitions: (1) text indicating end of term, (2) command indicating removal of a previously established ruling.
رفع (raf'): Removal; in the definition of naskh, it signifies the removal of the relation (talluq) of the ruling to the mukallaf, not the ruling itself.
بيان (bayan): Clarification; naskh as bayan means revealing the time limit that was always known to God.
أمد (amad): The appointed term or duration of a ruling, after which it expires.
تعلق (talluq): Relation; specifically the relation of the ruling to the mukallaf that makes the action obligatory.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi view presented in this passage, what does naskh (abrogation) essentially do?
A) It removes the ruling entirely from existence.
B) It clarifies the preordained time limit of the ruling.
C) It nullifies the ruling and replaces it with a contradictory one.
D) It abrogates the text of the Quran.

Key Takeaway
Naskh is, from the Hanafi perspective, a clarification of a preordained term, not a removal of an eternal ruling; the apparent removal is only of the relation to the mukallaf.

Naskh — Abrogation
intermediate
8 min
The Nature of Naskh: Raf‘ vs Bayan
Analyzing the two views on the essence of naskh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the two views on the definition of naskh and apply the Hanafi position that naskh has both raf‘ and bayan aspects.

Core Idea
Naskh is removal in our sight, but clarification of a pre-determined term in Allah's knowledge.

At first glance, naskh (abrogation) seems to replace a ruling with another, but usul scholars debate its essence: does it merely remove a ruling that would otherwise persist, or does it clarify a pre-set time limit? This passage unpacks the conceptual dispute, using the analogy of killing (qatl) to illustrate the difference between Mu‘tazila and Ahl al-Sunnah, and ultimately reconciles both perspectives within Hanafi usul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Locate the two competing definitions of naskh: raf‘ (removal of an implied perpetual ruling) and bayan (clarification of a pre-determined temporal limit). Understand the source of the dispute.
The passage states: 'النسخ رفع' vs. 'النسخ بيان'.
2
Analyze the analogy of killing
Examine how the Mu‘tazila and Ahl al-Sunnah use the example of a person killed. The Mu‘tazila argue the killing removes life that would have continued; Ahl al-Sunnah say the killed dies by his appointed term, and killing is only a sign. Map this to naskh: raf‘ vs. bayan.
The text: 'كالقتل عند المعتزلة... والثاني كالقتل عندنا معشر أهل السنة والجماعة'.
3
Understand the Hanafi reconciliation
Note that the Hanafi tradition, per Imam Fakhr al-Islam and al-Badi‘, holds that naskh has two aspects: from the Lawgiver's perspective it is bayan (clarification of the known term), from our perspective it is raf‘ (removal of the apparent continuation).
Key phrase: 'وهو في حق صاحب الشرع بيان محض... فكان تبديلاً في حقنا بياناً محضاً في حق صاحب الشرع'.
4
Apply the final definition
Learn al-Badi‘'s technical definition: 'انتهاء حكم شرعي مطلق عن التأييد والتوقيت بنص متأخر عن مورده' and understand the significance of the qualifications (absoluteness from perpetuity and time-fixing).
This definition avoids the extremes of absolute removal or pure clarification.
